<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genomic analyses of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus provide insights into asexual reproduction"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2017 49:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-04-10"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Qiang Xu and colleagues sequence four citrus species de novo, along with 100 accessions, including primitive, wild and cultivated citrus. Their genomic analyses associate the CitRWP gene with polyembryony and suggest that regions harboring energy- and reproduction-associated genes are probably under selection in cultivated citrus. The emergence of apomixis&#8212;the transition from sexual to asexual reproduction&#8212;is a prominent feature of modern citrus. Here we de novo sequenced and comprehensively studied the genomes of four representative citrus species. Additionally, we sequenced 100 accessions of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus. Comparative population analysis suggested that genomic regions harboring energy- and reproduction-associated genes are probably under selection in cultivated citrus. We also narrowed the genetic locus responsible for citrus polyembryony, a form of apomixis, to an 80-kb region containing 11 candidate genes. One of these, CitRWP, is expressed at higher levels in ovules of polyembryonic cultivars. We found a miniature inverted-repeat transposable element insertion in the promoter region of CitRWP that cosegregated with polyembryony. This study provides new insights into citrus apomixis and constitutes a promising resource for the mining of agriculturally important genes."/>

China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Lun-Wang" data-author-popup="auth-Lun-Wang">Lun Wang</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jidi-Xu" data-author-popup="auth-Jidi-Xu">Jidi Xu</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Xiaolin-Jiang" data-author-popup="auth-Xiaolin-Jiang">Xiaolin Jiang</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Zongzhou-Xie" data-author-popup="auth-Zongzhou-Xie">Zongzhou Xie</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Meilian-Tan" data-author-popup="auth-Meilian-Tan">Meilian Tan</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Robert_M-Larkin" data-author-popup="auth-Robert_M-Larkin">Robert M Larkin</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Ling_Ling-Chen" data-author-popup="auth-Ling_Ling-Chen">Ling-Ling Chen</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff3">3</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Hubei Key Laboratory of Agricultural Bioinformatics, College of Informatics, Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Hubei Key Laboratory of Agricultural Bioinformatics, College of Informatics, Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Yijun-Ruan" data-author-popup="auth-Yijun-Ruan">Yijun Ruan</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff5">5</a>,<a href="#Aff6">6</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="College of Life Science and Technology, Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, College of Life Science and Technology, Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Jackson Laboratory for Genomic Medicine" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.249880.f, 0000 0004 0374 0039, Jackson Laboratory for Genomic Medicine, Farmington, Connecticut, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Xiuxin-Deng" data-author-popup="auth-Xiuxin-Deng">Xiuxin Deng</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R. China" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Qiang-Xu" data-author-popup="auth-Qiang-Xu" data-corresp-id="c1">Qiang Xu<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="grid.35155.37, 0000 0004 1790 4137, Key Laboratory of Horticultural Plant Biology (Ministry of Education), Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, P.R.             <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>The emergence of apomixis—the transition from sexual to asexual reproduction—is a prominent feature of modern citrus. Here we <i>de novo</i> sequenced and comprehensively studied the genomes of four representative citrus species. Additionally, we sequenced 100 accessions of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus. Comparative population analysis suggested that genomic regions harboring energy- and reproduction-associated genes are probably under selection in cultivated citrus. We also narrowed the genetic locus responsible for citrus polyembryony, a form of apomixis, to an 80-kb region containing 11 candidate genes. One of these, <i>CitRWP</i>, is expressed at higher levels in ovules of polyembryonic cultivars. We found a miniature inverted-repeat transposable element insertion in the promoter region of <i>CitRWP</i> that cosegregated with polyembryony. This study provides new insights into citrus apomixis and constitutes a promising resource for the mining of agriculturally important genes.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Asexual reproduction is a remarkable feature of perennial fruit crops that facilitates the faithful propagation of commercially valuable individuals by avoiding the uncertainty associated with the sexual reproduction of hybrid plants. Many fruit crops reproduce or are propagated by asexual mechanisms. These mechanisms include natural means of propagation, such as layering, cutting and grafting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Meyer, R.S., DuVal, A.E. &amp; Jensen, H.R. Patterns and processes in crop domestication: an historical review and quantitative analysis of 203 global food crops. New Phytol. 196, 29–48 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d38365e733">1</a></sup>, and modern technologies, such as tissue culture. Apomixis is a naturally occurring mode of asexual reproduction that yields offspring that are genetically identical to the mother plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Conner, J.A., Mookkan, M., Huo, H., Chae, K. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. A parthenogenesis gene of apomict origin elicits embryo formation from unfertilized eggs in a sexual plant. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 112, 11205–11210 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d38365e737">2</a></sup>. It is uncommon in agriculturally important crops apart from citrus and apple<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Koltunow, A.M. Apomixis: embryo sacs and embryos formed without meiosis or fertilization in ovules. Plant Cell 5, 1425–1437 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d38365e741">3</a></sup>. The first example of apomixis in citrus was reported in 1719, when Leeuwenhoek observed the development of two plantlets from the same seed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Batygina, T.B. &amp; Vinogradova, G.Y. Phenomenon of polyembryony. Genetic heterogeneity of seeds. Russ. J. Dev. Biol. 38, 126–151 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d38365e745">4</a></sup>. A better understanding of apomixis and its introgression into agronomically important crops could potentially help revolutionize modern agriculture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Spillane, C., Curtis, M.D. &amp; Grossniklaus, U. Apomixis technology development—virgin births in farmers' fields? Nat. Biotechnol. 22, 687–691 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d38365e749">5</a></sup>. As demonstrated by fruit crops, apomixis enables breeders to fix valuable traits and heterozygosity. Most previous studies have focused on gametophytic apomixis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Conner, J.A., Mookkan, M., Huo, H., Chae, K. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. A parthenogenesis gene of apomict origin elicits embryo formation from unfertilized eggs in a sexual plant. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 112, 11205–11210 (2015)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d38365e754">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Bicknell, R.A. &amp; Koltunow, A.M. Understanding apomixis: recent advances and remaining conundrums. Plant Cell 16 (Suppl. 1), S228–S245 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d38365e757">6</a></sup>. Sporophytic apomixis, including nucellar embryony, is not as well studied.</p><p>Apomixis in citrus is sporophytic—the embryos develop from somatic nucellar cells—and very stable among commercial varieties, including sweet oranges, mandarins, grapefruits and lemons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kepiro, J.L. &amp; Roose, M.L. AFLP markers closely linked to a major gene essential for nucellar embryony (apomixis) in Citrus maxima × Poncirus trifoliata. Tree Genet. Genomes 6, 1–11 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38365e764">7</a></sup>. In general, 2–10 embryos develop within a seed. However, in particular genotypes, 30 or more embryos can develop in one seed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Koltunow, A.M. Apomixis: embryo sacs and embryos formed without meiosis or fertilization in ovules. Plant Cell 5, 1425–1437 (1993)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d38365e768">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Ueno, I., Iwamasa, M. &amp; Nishiura, M. Embryo number of various varieties of Citrus and its relatives. Bull. Hort. Res. Sta. Japan 7, 11–22 (1967)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d38365e771">8</a></sup>. This phenomenon is typically called 'polyembryony' and is considered an important trait for breeding purposes. On the one hand, polyembryony is widely employed in citrus nurseries and propagation programs to generate large numbers of uniform rootstocks from seeds. On the other hand, polyembryony has sometimes caused problems for breeding that required sexual crosses. Data from crosses between monoembryonic and polyembryonic cultivars indicate that one or two dominant loci control polyembryony<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Iwamasa, M., Ueno, I. &amp; Nishiura, M. Inheritance of nucellar embryony in citrus. Bull. Hort. Res. Sta. Japan 7, 1–8 (1967)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38365e775">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Cameron, J.W. &amp; Soost, R.K. Sexual and nucellar embryony in F1 hybrids and advanced crosses of Citrus with Poncirus. J. Am. Soc. Hortic. Sci. 104, 408–410 (1979)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d38365e778">10</a></sup>. Molecular markers linked to a polyembryony locus have been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Kepiro, J.L. &amp; Roose, M.L. AFLP markers closely linked to a major gene essential for nucellar embryony (apomixis) in Citrus maxima × Poncirus trifoliata. Tree Genet. Genomes 6, 1–11 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d38365e782">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Raga, V., Bernet, G.P., Carbonell, E.A. &amp; Asins, M.J. Segregation and linkage analyses in two complex populations derived from the citrus rootstock Cleopatra mandarin. Inheritance of seed reproductive traits. Tree Genet. Genomes 8, 1061–1071 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d38365e785">11</a></sup>. This locus was later mapped to a genomic region that spans approximately 380 kb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nakano, M. et al. Characterization of genomic sequence showing strong association with polyembryony among diverse Citrus species and cultivars, and its synteny with Vitis and Populus. Plant Sci. 183, 131–142 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38365e789">12</a></sup>. Genes differentially expressed during polyembryogenesis have also been identified<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Nakano, M. et al. Characterization of genes associated with polyembryony and in vitro somatic embryogenesis in Citrus. Tree Genet. Genomes 9, 795–803 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d38365e794">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kumar, V., Malik, S.K., Pal, D., Srinivasan, R. &amp; Bhat, S.R. Comparative transcriptome analysis of ovules reveals stress related genes associated with nucellar polyembryony in citrus. Tree Genet. Genomes 10, 449–464 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38365e797">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Long, J.M. et al. Genome-scale mRNA and small RNA transcriptomic insights into initiation of citrus apomixis. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 5743–5756 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38365e800">15</a></sup>.</p><p>Citrus fruit trees, designated as Citrinae, are a large group belonging to the subfamily Aurantioideae and the family Rutaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Swingle, W.T. &amp; Reece, P.C. The botany of Citrus and its wild relatives. in The Citrus Industry (University of California Press, 1967)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38365e807">16</a></sup>. Citrinae are classified into primitive citrus, near citrus and true citrus on the basis of botanical characteristics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Swingle, W.T. &amp; Reece, P.C. The botany of Citrus and its wild relatives. in The Citrus Industry (University of California Press, 1967)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d38365e811">16</a></sup>. <i>Atalantia buxifolia</i>, also known as Chinese box orange and formerly named <i>Severinia buxifolia</i>, is an example of a primitive citrus species. The true citrus group comprises almost all of the commercially cultivated citrus, with major varieties belonging to the <i>Citrus</i> genus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 1</a>). The best known citrus varieties are the sweet orange (<i>Citrus sinensis</i>), mandarin (<i>Citrus reticulata</i>), lemon (<i>Citrus limon</i>), grapefruit (<i>Citrus paradisi</i>) and pummelo (<i>Citrus grandis</i> or <i>Citrus maxima</i>). There are a range of reproductive systems among the different varieties of primitive and modern citrus species that include sexual reproduction, clonal propagation and mixtures of these systems.</p><p>We aimed to understand the genomic characteristics of primitive and cultivated citrus and the genetic basis of asexual reproduction in citrus. We <i>de novo</i> sequenced pummelo and three representative species of Citrinae. We also sequenced a population of 100 citrus accessions that ranged from primitive to cultivated citrus. Our genetic analyses of segregating and natural populations, and our transcriptome profiling, provide new insights into the genetic basis of apomixis in citrus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Citrinae phylogeny and reproductive systems</h3><p>Conserved single-copy genes from eight species of Citrinae were used for the construction of a phylogenetic tree (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). As expected, the primitive citrus <i>Atalantia</i> was located at a basal position in the tree. The spiny and wild species <i>Citrus ichangensis</i> (Ichang papeda, hereafter referred to as papeda) is phylogenetically located between primitive and cultivated citrus. The three basic species, <i>C. reticulata</i> (mandarin), <i>C. grandis</i> (pummelo) and <i>C. medica</i> (citron) showed close relationships to each other. Primitive, wild and cultivated citrus each have nine haploid chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Citrus species exhibit broad sexual compatibility. Interspecific and intergeneric hybridizations are feasible even between primitive and cultivated citrus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Medina-Filho, H.P., Bordignon, R. &amp; Ballvé, R.M.L. Sunkifolias and Buxisunkis: sexually obtained reciprocal hybrids of Citrus sunki × Severinia buxifolia. Genet. Mol. Biol. 21, 129–133 (1998)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d38365e887">17</a></sup>. Our experiments also support the grafting compatibility and partial sexual compatibility of <i>Atalantia</i> and <i>Citrus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Atalantia and papeda are mainly seed propagated, with large or many seeds that mostly fill the locules of the fruit (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>), and are not suitable for propagation by grafting owing to strong and numerous prickles and stiff twigs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>). In contrast, the vegetative propagule that facilitates propagation by grafting has an ideal morphology in mandarin, pummelo, sweet orange and other cultivated citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Phylogeny, genomes, fruits and seeds of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Phylogeny, genomes, fruits and seeds of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="552"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Citrinae phylogeny and genomes. A phylogenetic tree was constructed using the maximum likelihood method. Asterisks indicate the <i>de novo</i> genomes sequenced in this study. Circles indicate previously published genomes. Purple indicates primitive citrus; green indicates wild citrus; brown indicates the three basic species of citrus. Bootstrap values &gt;60 are indicated. (<b>b</b>) Differences among the fruits and seeds from primitive, wild and cultivated citrus. Cross-sections of young (left) and mature (right) fruit from different species are shown. The length of each line is based on the percentage of seed weight in the fruit. Scale bars, 1 cm.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">A high-quality and three draft genomes of Citrinae</h3><p>A high-quality genome of citrus was generated from haploid pummelo (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>) by single-molecule sequencing. A total of 56.8× coverage of single-molecule sequences on the PacBio RS II platform via a shotgun approach was used for genome assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Additionally, 370.3× Illumina sequencing data were used to correct sequencing errors and to fill in the gaps (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 2</a>). The contig N50 and N90 of the final assembly were 2.2 Mb and 70 kb, respectively. Moreover, the scaffold N50 and N90 were 4.2 Mb and 565 kb, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Tab1">Table 1</a>). The sequence contiguity represents 18-fold and a 44-fold improvements of the <i>C. clementina</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i> genomes, respectively, as evaluated by contig N50 (refs. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38365e968">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wu, G.A. et al. Sequencing of diverse mandarin, pummelo and orange genomes reveals complex history of admixture during citrus domestication. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 656–662 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38365e971">19</a>). Ninety percent of the assembly is in 101 scaffolds larger than 565 kb. The largest scaffold is 14.3 Mb. A total of 117 scaffolds, accounting for 87% of the assembled genome, were anchored by 1,563 molecular markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Figs. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics for the genome assembly of haploid pummelo</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3839/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Three draft genomes of heterozygous Citrinae species—atalantia (<i>A. buxifolia</i>, primitive citrus), papeda (<i>C. ichangensis</i>, wild citrus) and citron (<i>C. medica</i>) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>)—were sequenced and assembled with Illumina shotgun sequencing reads. High-coverage sequencing data that ranged from 164.4× to 401.9× were produced from multiple libraries, with small insert sizes that ranged from 140 bp to 500 bp and long insert sizes of 2 kb, 5 kb and 20 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Tables 4–6</a>). The sequenced reads were assembled using the de Bruijn graph algorithm from the SOAPdenovo package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1290">20</a></sup>, resulting in scaffold N50 values of 1,074 kb, 504 kb and 367 kb (with contig N50 values of 23.9 kb, 76.6 kb and 46.5 kb) for atalantia, papeda and citron, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 7</a>).</p><p><i>Ab initio</i> gene prediction programs, homology searches and RNA-seq analysis were integrated to annotate the pummelo genome. We identified 30,123 protein-coding genes with 42,886 transcripts. The gene transcripts had an average length of 1,572 bp, a mean coding sequence size of 1,141 bp and an average exon length of 277 bp. RNA-seq data revealed that 12,763 gene loci (42.4%) encoded two or more transcriptional isoforms. Regarding the core and dispensable portions of these genomes, all of the genes in the six genomes (29,655 in sweet orange; 24,533 in clementine mandarin; 30,123 in pummelo; 32,579 in citron; 32,067 in papeda; 28,420 in atalantia) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 7</a>) were classified into 23,829 gene families on the basis of the homology of their encoded proteins. A total of 12,457 (52%) gene families were shared by all six of these genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Further analysis indicated a similar number of genes in almost all of the transcription factor gene families in the six citrus genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Tables 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genomic characteristics of citrus</h3><p>The four sequenced species from this study (atalantia, papeda, citron and pummelo), and the two previously published sequenced species (sweet orange and clementine mandarin)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1325">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wu, G.A. et al. Sequencing of diverse mandarin, pummelo and orange genomes reveals complex history of admixture during citrus domestication. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 656–662 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1328">19</a></sup> range from primitive to cultivated citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). An overview of the genome synteny and sequence variation is presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig2">Figure 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Figures 9–13</a>. The genome sizes are similar among the <i>Citrus</i> species. We observed the most variation in genome size between <i>Atalantia</i> and the <i>Citrus</i> species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 2</a>). On the basis of our presence–absence variation (PAV) analysis, 33.28% of the unique regions in pummelo (relative to atalantia) contained transposable elements (TEs), and 2.68% of the unique regions contained protein-coding genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 12</a>). Approximately one-fifth of the genes in the unique regions (153 of 780 annotated genes) of pummelo were predicted to have known functions. These genes were significantly enriched (<i>P</i> value &lt; 0.05 and FDR &lt; 0.05) in three GO terms: defense response and proteolysis (biological process category) and pectate lyase activity (molecular function category) relative to the entire genome of pummelo (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>). Among the 153 genes with functional annotations, 93 are homologous to one or more genes (60.78%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 13</a>). Conversely, the unique regions in atalantia relative to pummelo had a higher TE content (54.20%) than the entire atalantia genome (43.55%).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Characteristics of the citrus genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Characteristics of the citrus genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="340"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The distribution of the syntenic regions and the confident SNVs between the pummelo genome and the three genomes representing cultivated, wild and primitive citrus are shown. The lines in the center of the circle indicate pairs of homologous genes on the different chromosomes of pummelo.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>SNVs were confidently identified by sequence alignment (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>) and filtered using resequencing data. A pairwise comparison of each species versus pummelo showed that the transition/transversion ratios varied from 1.38 to 1.66, with an average of 1.57 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 14</a>). The distributions of SNVs in gene structures displayed the highest density in the 5′ and 3′ UTRs (an average of 18.8 and 17.2 SNVs per kb for 5′ and 3′ UTRs, respectively). The second highest density of SNVs was in introns (an average of 14.2 SNVs per kb). The lowest density of SNVs was in coding sequence (CDS) regions (an average of 9.7 SNVs per kb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 15</a>).</p><p>The ratio of nonsynonymous to synonymous substitutions (dN/dS) was calculated for each of the 8,551 single-copy orthologs shared by these six citrus genomes. We found that 207, 100, 104, 66, 106 and 77 genes showed higher dN/dS values in atalantia, papeda, citron, pummelo, clementine mandarin and sweet orange (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 16</a>), respectively, relative to other species. For the dN/dS of single-copy orthologous genes, 12.79% of the genes showed a value greater than one in atalantia (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 17</a>). This proportion was higher than the average value of 6.82% from other citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Tables 18–22</a>), indicating greater sequence divergence in atalantia relative to other citrus. These genes may have functional associations with the biological features of atalantia. For instance, <i>Cg4g018790</i> encodes a cytokinin oxidase/dehydrogenase homologous to the rice <i>OsCKX2</i> gene, which has been reported to control seed number<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ashikari, M. et al. Cytokinin oxidase regulates rice grain production. Science 309, 741–745 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1420">21</a></sup>. In this gene, higher sequence polymorphisms were observed in atalantia relative to other citrus species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Genetic diversity and comparative population analysis</h3><p>Citrinae species exhibit high levels of morphological diversity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>), which is a reflection of their diverse genetic backgrounds. We selected and sequenced 100 citrus accessions with an average depth of 30-fold genome coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 23</a>). Sequencing data from three mandarins, one orange and four pummelos from previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1441">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wu, G.A. et al. Sequencing of diverse mandarin, pummelo and orange genomes reveals complex history of admixture during citrus domestication. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 656–662 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1444">19</a></sup> were also analyzed. This set of 108 citrus accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 17</a>) was used for population diversity analysis, genetic differentiation analysis and association analysis of the apomixis trait. For comparative population analysis, 15 atalantia accessions, 11 papeda accessions and 19 pummelo accessions, representing primitive, wild and cultivated citrus, were used (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 18</a>). The fixation index (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) values among these citrus populations fluctuated around 0.28 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 19</a>), which indicates moderate genetic differentiations among citrus populations. For a comparison, the <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> was 0.34 between two wild populations of common bean and 0.44 between wild and cultivated cucumber<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1469">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Qi, J. et al. A genomic variation map provides insights into the genetic basis of cucumber domestication and diversity. Nat. Genet. 45, 1510–1515 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1472">23</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genetic diversity and population differentiation analysis of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Genetic diversity and population differentiation analysis of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="295"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The diversity of citrus germplasms in this study. Inner circle, primitive citrus; middle circle, wild and cultivated citrus; outer circle, cultivated citrus. (<b>b</b>) Maximum likelihood phylogenetic tree of 45 citrus accessions (15 atalantia, 11 papeda and 19 pummelo) used in the comparative population analysis. Bootstrap values are indicated in the tree. (<b>c</b>) The distribution of π along the chromosomes among the populations of atalantia, papeda and pummelo, respectively. These values were calculated in 2-Mb sliding windows with 1-Mb steps. (<b>d</b>) Distribution of the reduction of diversity (log<sub>10</sub> π ratios) and differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values) from the pairwise comparison of the atalantia and pummelo populations. π ratios were calculated as π<sub>atalantia</sub>/π<sub>pummelo</sub> in 50-kb sliding windows with 10-kb steps. The region above the dashed line in the distribution of log<sub>10</sub> π ratios corresponds to the 5% right tail of the empirical distribution (log<sub>10</sub> π ratio = 0.36). The regions above the dashed line in the <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values distribution are in the 5% right tail of the empirical distribution (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> is 0.40).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Nucleotide diversity (π) analysis indicated that atalantia has the highest genetic diversity and that papeda has a higher level of genetic diversity than pummelo (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>). We observed a dramatic reduction in genetic diversity between atalantia and papeda. Additional sequence analysis indicated that atalantia has more highly diverged regions (threefold more highly divergent regions, on average) than the other two populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 24</a>). For example, a long region at the end of chromosome 5 (39.64–49.38 Mb, about one-fifth of the chromosome) displayed the highest diversity in atalantia and a dramatic reduction in diversity in papeda and pummelo (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3c</a>).</p><p>Genomic regions that showed both high differentiation and reduced diversity were identified among the primitive, wild and cultivated citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 20</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Tables 25–27</a>). The results of pairwise comparisons indicated that the distribution of such regions was uneven along the chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 21</a>). On the basis of the pairwise comparison between atalantia and pummelo, we concluded that a total of 18.1 Mb of genomic regions, containing 2,430 genes, were probably under selection in pummelo (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 28</a>). These regions include genes associated with vegetative growth, such as <i>Cg7g021010, Cg4g017400</i> and <i>Cg9g010410</i>, which are homologous to <i>TCP15</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Kieffer, M., Master, V., Waites, R. &amp; Davies, B. TCP14 and TCP15 affect internode length and leaf shape in Arabidopsis. Plant J. 68, 147–158 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1575">24</a>), <i>LAS</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Greb, T. et al. Molecular analysis of the LATERAL SUPPRESSOR gene in Arabidopsis reveals a conserved control mechanism for axillary meristem formation. Genes Dev. 17, 1175–1187 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1582">25</a></sup> and <i>YUCCA3</i> (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhao, Y. et al. A role for flavin monooxygenase–like enzymes in auxin biosynthesis. Science 291, 306–309 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1588">26</a>), respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 22</a>). Notably, we observed a strong signal on chromosome 9, spanning 220 kb, from 13.8 Mb to 14.0 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig3">Fig. 3d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 23</a>) in the pummelo genome. Thirteen of the fifteen annotated genes in this region have functions related to cytochrome <i>c</i> biogenesis, mitochondrial proteins, ATP synthase, NADH dehydrogenase and mitochondrial ribosomal proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 25</a>). Comparative analysis also indicated that flowering-related genes, such as the gene encoding the WUSCHEL-related homeobox (WOX)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="van der Graaff, E., Laux, T. &amp; Rensing, S.A. The WUS homeobox-containing (WOX) protein family. Genome Biol. 10, 248 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1608">27</a></sup> transcription factor (<i>Cg4g011660</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 24</a>) and the gene encoding the flowering time control protein FPA (<i>Cg2g027110</i>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 25</a>), showed strongly reduced diversity and a high degree of differentiation. Therefore, we suggest that these genes were probably under selection. These regions were shown to harbor signals of selection as defined by recent studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Schmutz, J. et al. A reference genome for common bean and genome-wide analysis of dual domestications. Nat. Genet. 46, 707–713 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1625">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Lin, T. et al. Genomic analyses provide insights into the history of tomato breeding. Nat. Genet. 46, 1220–1226 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1628">28</a></sup>. The effect caused by other possibilities, such as genetic drift and bottlenecks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wolf, J.B.W. &amp; Ellegren, H. Making sense of genomic islands of differentiation in light of speciation. Nat. Rev. Genet. 18, 87–100 (2017)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1632">29</a></sup>, could not be excluded but might be limited, as indicated by the similar pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent (PSMC) curve of these three populations (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 26</a>). <i>FPA</i> has a critical role in the regulation of flowering time in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schomburg, F.M., Patton, D.A., Meinke, D.W. &amp; Amasino, R.M. FPA, a gene involved in floral induction in Arabidopsis, encodes a protein containing RNA-recognition motifs. Plant Cell 13, 1427–1436 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1645">30</a></sup>. This finding is consistent with the observation that atalantia has a prolonged flowering season (from May to December) and that in contrast, the flowering season is synchronized among all of the other cultivated citrus.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">The genomic basis of citrus apomixis (nucellar polyembryony)</h3><p>The emergence of a particular type of apomixis, nucellar polyembryony, in mandarin and most cultivated varieties of citrus is one of the prominent features of the recent evolution of citrus (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). We scored the polyembryony phenotypes of 124 fruit-yielding progeny from a segregating population derived from a cross between HB pummelo (monoembryony) and Fairchild mandarin (polyembryony) that exhibited contrasting phenotypes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 29</a>). For the bulk segregant analysis (BSA), the transformed Δ(SNP index) was calculated and plotted against the genomic sequence in 250-kb sliding windows with step sizes of 10 kb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). The peak value for the transformed Δ(SNP index) was localized to a region spanning from 23.695 to 25.655 Mb on chromosome 4. These data indicated that this 1.96-Mb region harbors a major locus contributing to nucellar polyembryony.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Genetic mapping of the citrus polyembryony."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Genetic mapping of the citrus polyembryony.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="259"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The phenotype of citrus polyembryony. (<b>b</b>) Mapping the polyembryony by bulk segregant analysis (BSA) of a segregating population derived from HB pummelo × Fairchild mandarin cross. Red arrow indicates the position of the 1.96-Mb peak. The transformed Δ(SNP index) is the product of the Δ(SNP index) and normalized SNP density in each 250-kb sliding window (10-kb steps). (<b>c</b>) Gene-based local association analysis in the relevant region. Top, heat map for association levels in the relevant region. The −log<sub>10</sub>-transformed <i>P</i> (two-tailed Fisher's exact test) was calculated in each 50-kb sliding window (10-kb steps). Middle, the 80-kb candidate region with the strongest association. Regression line (red) and confidence intervals (gray) were calculated using a linear model at a confidence level of 0.95. Bottom, genes in the candidate region in diploid pummelo. Boxes represent genes; horizontal black line represents intergenic regions; vertical lines show positions of 5′ end of the genes. Genes annotated from the haploid pummelo genome are presented in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Figure 28</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To fine map the polyembryony locus, a local association analysis with the variant information from the protein-encoding genes in the 1.96 Mb region was performed using the genome sequencing data from the 108 aforementioned accessions, including 45 polyembryonic and 63 monoembryonic accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 23</a>). We employed <i>P</i> values from the two-tailed Fisher's exact test to measure the correlations between nucleotide variation and phenotype (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 27</a>), which were adjusted with a false discovery rate (FDR) correction for multiple testing. This analysis localized the polyembryony locus to an <span class="stix">∼</span>80-kb region on chromosome 4, between 24.54 and 24.62 Mb, that contains 11 genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 28</a>). Consistent with this result, the population differentiation between the polyembryonic and monoembryonic accessions was substantially higher for this 80-kb region than the surrounding regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 29</a>). The linkage disequilibrium (LD) among polyembryonic accessions was larger than the LD among monoembryonic accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 29</a>), which indicates a selection signature between monoembryonic and polyembryonic citrus.</p><p>Among the 11 candidate genes, <i>Cg4g018910</i> and <i>Cg4g018970</i> showed the highest association with the polyembryony phenotype (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). Expression analysis of all candidate genes indicated that <i>Cg4g018970</i> was highly expressed in ovules and specifically expressed in polyembryonic citrus cultivars but not in monoembryonic citrus cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig5">Fig. 5b,c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 30</a>). <i>Cg4g018970</i> encodes a RWP-RK domain–containing protein similar to the <i>Arabidopsis</i> RKD family of proteins, which serve as regulators of egg cell–related genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Köszegi, D. et al. Members of the RKD transcription factor family induce an egg cell-like gene expression program. Plant J. 67, 280–291 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1758">31</a></sup>. Thus, we hereafter refer to <i>Cg4g018970</i> as <i>CitRWP</i>. A sequence alignment of <i>CitRWP</i> from polyembryonic and monoembryonic citrus identified four SNPs in the gene sequence and an insertion of a miniature inverted-repeat TE (MITE) in the promoter region (g.24610316_24610317ins(203) in chromosome 4 in the reference of pummelo genome (MKYQ00000000)) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 31</a>). To test whether this transposon is linked to the polyembryony locus, we screened the 124 fruit-yielding individuals from the segregating population of 217 progeny derived from an HB pummelo × Fairchild mandarin cross. We did not find this MITE insertion in any of the monoembryonic progeny (<i>n</i> = 66). In contrast, we found the MITE insertion in all of the polyembryonic progeny (<i>n</i> = 58) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 32</a>). Thus, we conclude that this MITE insertion is associated with the polyembryony locus. Next, we used a germplasm collection containing 786 accessions of citrus to independently test this association. Consistent with our results, we found this MITE insertion in all of the polyembryonic accessions (<i>n</i> = 213). In contrast, all the monoembryonic accessions (<i>n</i> = 573) lack this particular MITE insertion (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig5">Fig. 5e,f</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 30</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Candidate genes associated with citrus polyembryony."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Candidate genes associated with citrus polyembryony.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="317"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Beeswarm and box plot for associated SNPs in the 11 candidate genes. The dots in different colors represent associated SNPs in the corresponding gene sequence. Boxes, median and interquartile range; whiskers maximum and minimum values. (<b>b</b>) Relative expression of <i>CitRWP</i> as determined by qRT-PCR in different tissues of sweet orange. Data are mean ± s.d.; <i>n</i> = 3 technical replicates of 2 pooled tissue samples. (<b>c</b>) qRT-PCR validation of <i>CitRWP</i> expression in the ovules of monoembryonic cultivars (PML, pummelo; CTR, citron; CLM, clementine mandarin) and polyembryonic cultivars (GRP, grapefruit; LM, lemon; PK, ponkan). Data are mean ± s.d.; <i>n</i> = 3 technical replicates of 2 pooled tissue samples. (<b>d</b>) Schematic diagram of <i>CitRWP</i>. Asterisks indicate positions of SNPs associated with polyembryony; triangle indicates MITE insertion; orange rectangles indicate exons. (<b>e</b>) Representative PCR-based detection of MITE insertions in citrus cultivars. (<b>f</b>) PCR-based analysis of the MITE insertion in 786 citrus accessions and the F<sub>1</sub> segregating population derived from the HB pummelo (HB) × Fairchild mandarin (FC) cross.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Transcriptome of sexual and nucellar embryos from citrus</h3><p>To investigate the molecular processes and genes involved in sexual and nucellar embryogenesis in citrus, we compared the RNA-seq data derived from leaves, ovules, fruits and seeds of pummelo (sexual type) and sweet orange (asexual type). We found that 1,637 and 1,840 genes were highly expressed in ovules (≥2-fold higher than in other tissues) of pummelo and sweet orange, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig6">Fig. 6a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Tables 31</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">32</a>). We found that 853 of these genes were highly expressed in both pummelo and sweet orange. Gene Ontology (GO) analysis indicated that the genes associated with microtubule-based movement, DNA packaging, cell cycle, DNA replication, mitosis and transcription regulator activity were over-represented among the genes that were highly expressed in ovules (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 33</a>). The genes that were highly expressed in the ovules of sweet orange were significantly (P &lt; 0.05; FDR &lt; 0.05) enriched in almost the same gene ontologies as the group of 853 genes that were highly expressed in the ovules of both pummelo and sweet orange, such as regulation of transcription, mitosis, nuclear division and mitotic cell cycle, which contribute to cell division (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 34</a>). These data highlight similarities in the transcriptomes of nucellar and sexual embryos.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Comparative transcriptome analysis of monoembryonic and polyembryonic citrus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Comparative transcriptome analysis of monoembryonic and polyembryonic citrus.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.3839/MediaObjects/41588_2017_Article_BFng3839_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="530"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Heat map of genes that are specifically expressed in ovules of both pummelo (monoembryony) and sweet orange (polyembryony). Leaf, ovule, fruit and seed tissues from pummelo (odd numbers) and sweet orange (even numbers) were used for the comparative analysis. (<b>b</b>) Heat map of the genes with significantly different expression in the ovules of monoembryonic (PML, pummelo; CTR, citron; CLM, clementine mandarin) and polyembryonic cultivars (GRP, grapefruit; LM, lemon; PK, ponkan). Data are derived from 2 replicates of plant tissue. Normalized FPKM values after scaling and centering are shown. (<b>c</b>) Model for genes that are involved in monoembryony and polyembryony, including the candidate gene <i>CitRWP</i> (yellow star). Blue dots indicate genes highly expressed in ovules and showed no significant difference in expression between monoembryonic and polyembryonic citrus cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Tables 36</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">37</a>). Red dots indicate genes highly expressed in the ovules of polyembryonic citrus cultivars. M, megaspore mother cell; ES, embryo sac; N, nucellar embryo initiation cell.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.3839/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To identify genes that are differentially expressed between polyembryony and monoembryony, a comparative transcriptome analysis was performed on the ovules from three pairs of monoembryonic and polyembryonic citrus cultivars (pummelo–grapefruit, citron–lemon, clementine–ponkan) at the key stage of nucellar embryo initiation (i.e., 3 d before anthesis)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kumar, V., Malik, S.K., Pal, D., Srinivasan, R. &amp; Bhat, S.R. Comparative transcriptome analysis of ovules reveals stress related genes associated with nucellar polyembryony in citrus. Tree Genet. Genomes 10, 449–464 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1923">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Long, J.M. et al. Genome-scale mRNA and small RNA transcriptomic insights into initiation of citrus apomixis. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 5743–5756 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1926">15</a></sup>. Considering the genetic differences between each pair of citrus cultivars, we performed a pairwise comparison of the three pairs of RNA-seq data. Among the 2,624 genes that were preferentially expressed in the ovules of pummelo and sweet orange, 29 showed markedly different (≥2-fold) expression levels between each pair of monoembryonic and polyembryonic cultivars (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig6">Fig. 6b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 35</a>). The annotation of these genes and analysis of homologous <i>Arabidopsis</i> sequences indicated that 11 of the 29 genes encode proteins with unknown function. Almost half of the remaining genes were associated with the biological processes of stress response (<i>RNS1, ATSOD1, Cg4g011120</i> and <i>Cg9g004560</i>) and oxidation reduction (<i>ATBBE18</i> and <i>Cg3g004030</i>).
