Three additional genes (<i>EDA24, AGL2</i> and <i>ACR4</i>) contribute to flower and embryo development in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and other plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Pagnussat, G.C. et al. Genetic and molecular identification of genes required for female gametophyte development and function in Arabidopsis. Development 132, 603–614 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1962">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Dreni, L. &amp; Zhang, D. Flower development: the evolutionary history and functions of the AGL6 subfamily MADS-box genes. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 1625–1638 (2016)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1965">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Gifford, M.L., Dean, S. &amp; Ingram, G.C. The Arabidopsis ACR4 gene plays a role in cell layer organisation during ovule integument and sepal margin development. Development 130, 4249–4258 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1968">34</a></sup>. These genes are expressed in the ovules of both monoembryonic and polyembryonic cultivars and are expressed at dramatically higher levels in polyembryonic cultivars. <i>CitRWP</i> shows a similar expression trend in polyembryonic cultivars, which is consistent with our conclusion, from genetic analysis, that <i>CitRWP</i> is the key candidate gene controlling polyembryony (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig6">Fig. 6c</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Tables 36</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">37</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>We sequenced and assembled one high-quality genome and three draft genomes of Citrinae species. The haploid pummelo genome represents the most contiguous citrus genome assembly to date. The sequence contiguity (contig N50) of the long reads assembly using PacBio data is at least 18-fold higher than that of recently published citrus genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1996">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wu, G.A. et al. Sequencing of diverse mandarin, pummelo and orange genomes reveals complex history of admixture during citrus domestication. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 656–662 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38365e1999">19</a></sup>. The shotgun approach using PacBio single-molecule sequencing in combination with Illumina data is effective and efficient for a <i>de novo</i> genome assembly. This high-quality pummelo genome together with the three draft genomes of citron, papeda and atalantia constitute a valuable platform for citrus genetic and genomic research.</p><p>Change in the reproductive system is one of the striking features in the evolution and domestication of fruit crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Miller, A.J. &amp; Gross, B.L. From forest to field: perennial fruit crop domestication. Am. J. Bot. 98, 1389–1414 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2009">35</a></sup>. Consistent with this observation, the sequence analysis of the six genomes that included primitive, wild and cultivated citrus indicated frequent variations in flowering- and seed-related genes, such as <i>CKX</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ashikari, M. et al. Cytokinin oxidase regulates rice grain production. Science 309, 741–745 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2015">21</a></sup>. The comparative population analysis of these species indicated that mitochondria- and reproduction-associated genes, such as <i>WOX</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="van der Graaff, E., Laux, T. &amp; Rensing, S.A. The WUS homeobox-containing (WOX) protein family. Genome Biol. 10, 248 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2021">27</a></sup> and <i>FPA</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schomburg, F.M., Patton, D.A., Meinke, D.W. &amp; Amasino, R.M. FPA, a gene involved in floral induction in Arabidopsis, encodes a protein containing RNA-recognition motifs. Plant Cell 13, 1427–1436 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2027">30</a></sup>, are probably under selection in cultivated citrus.</p><p>Apomixis has received considerable attention because of its capability to permanently fix valuable traits and hybrid genotypes (hybrid vigor). It is possible that apomixis was used inadvertently during the history of citrus breeding and selection. When desirable variations and traits were observed, the apomictic lines tended to have a greater chance of being selected, maintained and dispersed. This may also explain why most of the commercial and elite citrus are apomictic. In this study, we provided a comprehensive analysis of citrus apomixis (polyembryony) using genetic, genomic and transcriptomic approaches. The segregation ratios of both the phenotype and genotype in a segregating F<sub>1</sub> population are consistent with the single dominant gene model for nucellar embryony<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Iwamasa, M., Ueno, I. &amp; Nishiura, M. Inheritance of nucellar embryony in citrus. Bull. Hort. Res. Sta. Japan 7, 1–8 (1967)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2036">9</a></sup>. The cosegregation of the MITE insertion in the promoter region of <i>CitRWP</i> with the polyembryonic phenotype lends more support to the single dominant mutation model for the emergence of apomixis in mandarin. Our genomic analysis localized the candidate region to an 80-kb interval containing 11 genes, which was embedded in a 380-kb region reported by a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Nakano, M. et al. Characterization of genomic sequence showing strong association with polyembryony among diverse Citrus species and cultivars, and its synteny with Vitis and Populus. Plant Sci. 183, 131–142 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2043">12</a></sup>. In this interval, we identified a promising candidate gene for the single dominant allele responsible for polyembryony in citrus. Notably, this candidate gene, <i>CitRWP</i>, is specifically expressed in ovules and at higher levels in polyembryonic cultivars. A gene harboring the same domain, <i>AtRKD4</i> (<i>GROUNDED</i>), was reported to promote embryogenesis in somatic cells when transiently overexpressed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jeong, S., Palmer, T.M. &amp; Lukowitz, W. The RWP-RK factor GROUNDED promotes embryonic polarity by facilitating YODA MAP kinase signaling. Curr. Biol. 21, 1268–1276 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2057">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Waki, T., Hiki, T., Watanabe, R., Hashimoto, T. &amp; Nakajima, K. The Arabidopsis RWP-RK protein RKD4 triggers gene expression and pattern formation in early embryogenesis. Curr. Biol. 21, 1277–1281 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2060">37</a></sup>. Fully elucidating the contribution of <i>CitRWP</i> to apomixis will require knocking out <i>CitRWP</i> in citrus.</p><p>Our transcriptome analyses provide global insights into the molecular processes involved in the development of sexual and asexual (nucellar) embryos in citrus. The results indicate a close developmental relationship between sexual reproduction and apomixis in citrus, which is consistent with a report on apomictic <i>Boechera gunnisoniana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schmidt, A. et al. Apomictic and sexual germline development differ with respect to cell cycle, transcriptional, hormonal and epigenetic regulation. PLoS Genet. 10, e1004476 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2075">38</a></sup>. The unusually high expression of three sexual embryogenesis–associated genes during apomixis (<i>AGL2, EDA24</i> and <i>ACR4</i>) provides more support for the notion that nucellar embryogenesis and sexual reproduction may utilize similar genes or pathways. Previous studies reported that extreme stress induces the initiation of autonomous embryo development in somatic cells in response to exogenous or endogenous signals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Pasternak, T.P. et al. The role of auxin, pH, and stress in the activation of embryogenic cell division in leaf protoplast-derived cells of alfalfa. Plant Physiol. 129, 1807–1819 (2002)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2085">39</a></sup>. Here we identified six nucellar embryo-associated genes that are regulated by stress, which is in agreement with a previous report on apomixis in <i>Hieracium praealtum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Okada, T. et al. Enlarging cells initiating apomixis in Hieracium praealtum transition to an embryo sac program prior to entering mitosis. Plant Physiol. 163, 216–231 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2092">40</a></sup>.</p><p>This genomic information on primitive, wild and cultivated citrus may also be valuable for the future analysis of metabolism and to further investigate the basis of historical uses of citrus species. For example, <i>C. medica</i> was historically used for medicinal purposes, and atalantia was also used as a traditional medicinal plant for folk medicines aiming to treat malaria, chronic rheumatism, paralysis and snakebites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Shi, M., Guo, X., Chen, Y., Zhou, L. &amp; Zhang, D. Isolation and characterization of 19 polymorphic microsatellite loci for Atalantia buxifolia (Rutaceae), a traditional medicinal plant. Conserv. Genet. Resour. 6, 857–859 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2103">41</a></sup>. Additionally, wild and primitive citrus, which have been largely neglected during the history of citrus breeding, exhibit higher genetic diversity that is useful for managing disease in cultivated citrus with narrow genetic backgrounds resulting from apomixis, clonal propagation and human selection. Future characterization and utilization of genes controlling apomixis, metabolism, disease resistance and other agriculturally important traits is a promising path for the improvement of crop breeding.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant materials.</h3><p>A total of 100 citrus accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 23</a>) were sequenced in this study. The germplasm containing 786 citrus accessions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 30</a>) was collected at the National Citrus Breeding Center, Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, China, and the Citrus Experiment Stations from the MATS program of the Ministry of Agriculture of China. The haploid pummelo was obtained from the Citrus Research Institute, Southwest University, Chongqing, China (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>).</p><p>The segregating population derived from the HB pummelo × Fairchild mandarin cross comprises 217 individuals including 124 fruit-bearing progeny (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 29</a>). The population was created in 2004 and began flowering in 2012 at the National Citrus Breeding Center, Huazhong Agricultural University, Wuhan, China.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12"><i>De novo</i> assembly and annotation of four citrus genomes.</h3><p>For the haploid <i>C. grandis</i>, 30 cells of PacBio data (56.8 × genome coverage) and 141 Gb of Illumina data (370.3 × genome coverage; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Tables 1, 2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">38</a>) containing both short and long inserts were used. For the assembly of haploid pummelo genome, the PacBio long reads were corrected and assembled using the Hierarchical Genome Assembly Process (HGAP)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Chin, C.S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2154">42</a></sup> in SMRT Analysis (v2.3.0). Another round of polishing was performed with Quiver<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Chin, C.S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563–569 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2158">42</a></sup> to further improve the quality of the assembled contigs. Next, mate-paired reads corrected by Quake<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Kelley, D.R., Schatz, M.C. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. Quake: quality-aware detection and correction of sequencing errors. Genome Biol. 11, R116 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2163">43</a></sup> were used in scaffolding with the SSPACE-STANDARD package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C.V., Jansen, H.J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2167">44</a></sup>, and GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2171">20</a></sup> was used to fill the gaps with all of the Illumina reads. Then, the SSPACE-STANDARD package was used to further extend the gap-filled scaffolds. To improve the completeness of the assembly, long contigs assembled only with Illumina reads were aligned to the PacBio-based assembly, and the unaligned sequences were added to the final assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 3</a>).</p><p>For the heterozygous diploid <i>A. buxifolia, C. ichangensis</i> and <i>C. medica</i>, short-insert (i.e., insert sizes ranging from 140 bp to 500 bp) and long-insert (i.e., insert sizes of 2, 5, and 20 kb) DNA libraries were constructed (Illumina) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Tables 4–6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">38</a>). In total, 131.9 Gb (<span class="stix">∼</span>401.9× genome coverage), 64.3 Gb (<span class="stix">∼</span>164.4× genome coverage), and 69.5 Gb (<span class="stix">∼</span>170.9× genome coverage) of raw data were generated for <i>A. buxifolia, C. ichangensis</i> and <i>C. medica</i>, respectively. The draft genomes were assembled using SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2200">20</a></sup> with Illumina reads corrected using QUAKE. Then, all the reads were aligned onto the contigs for scaffolding, and the GapCloser package was used to fill the gaps. For the atalantia genome, the 3.73 Gb PacBio reads were corrected using the Illumina reads and were then used to improve the assembly. For the annotation of each of the four genomes, the TEs were masked and the annotation was performed <i>ab initio</i> by integrating the evidence from sequence homology and transcriptomic data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Notes 4–6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 39</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Phylogenetic tree of Citrinae.</h3><p>The coding region sequences from 103 single-copy genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2221">18</a></sup> were concatenated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM103">Supplementary Data Set 1</a>), constrained to their amino acid sequences and used for tree construction. The maximum likelihood (ML) tree was produced with Clausena as the outgroup using the substitution model GTRGAMMA of the RAxML software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics 30, 1312–1313 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2228">45</a></sup>. A total of 1,000 rapid bootstrap inferences were performed.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Detection of single nucleotide variations (SNVs) and presence–absence variations (PAVs).</h3><p>The six citrus genomes (<i>A. buxifolia, C. ichangensis, C. medica, C. grandis, C. clementina</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i>) were used for comparison and synteny analyses. The differences between pairs of genomes were found using the Nucmer program in the MUMmer software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2246">46</a></sup> with the default parameters. Alignment results of the five query genome sequences (<i>A. buxifolia, C. ichangensis, C. medica, C. clementina</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i>) against the reference genome of <i>C. grandis</i> were used to extract SNVs, PAVs and indels (insertions and deletions), which were further filtered and validated with re-sequencing data. In addition, the enrichment analysis for genes in the unique regions was performed using agriGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Du, Z., Zhou, X., Ling, Y., Zhang, Z. &amp; Su, Z. agriGO: a GO analysis toolkit for the agricultural community. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, W64–W70 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2260">47</a></sup> against the background of the corresponding whole genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 7</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genomic characterization of six citrus genomes.</h3><p>Protein-encoding genes from <i>A. buxifolia, C. ichangensis, C. medica, C. grandis, C. clementina</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i> were compared using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Altschul, S.F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E.W. &amp; Lipman, D.J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2282">48</a></sup> with an <i>E</i>-value cutoff of 1 × 10<sup>−10</sup> and clustered using OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178–2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2292">49</a></sup>. The results of all-by-all BLASTP was used to estimate the synteny for pairs of citrus genomes with the i-ADHoRe software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Simillion, C., Janssens, K., Sterck, L. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. i-ADHoRe 2.0: an improved tool to detect degenerated genomic homology using genomic profiles. Bioinformatics 24, 127–128 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2296">50</a></sup>. The gene family members in each genome were identified using the HMMER software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Finn, R.D., Clements, J. &amp; Eddy, S.R. HMMER web server: interactive sequence similarity searching. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, W29–W37 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2300">51</a></sup>, on the basis of the domain profiles of 62 transcription factor families, 22 groups of transcriptional regulators collected in the PlnTFDB database (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Sec24">URLs</a>) and the NB-ARC domain (PF00931) from Pfam (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Sec24">URLs</a>). The domains of the proteins encoded by the members of the MADS-box (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM104">Supplementary Data Set 2</a>) and NBS-related genes were manually checked using PROSITE (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Sec24">URLs</a>). The MADS-box and NBS-related genes were classified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Figs. 33</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">34</a>).</p><p>The dN/dS values were calculated for each of the 8,551 single-copy orthologous genes shared by the six citrus genomes using the codeml program in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2326">52</a></sup> with the one-ratio branch model and the free-ratios branch model. Using a likelihood ratio test, the values of dN/dS for each single-copy orthologous gene were determined and the fast-evolving single-copy orthologous genes were identified for each citrus genome (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 and FDR &lt; 0.05).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genetic diversity and <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> analysis.</h3><p>A total of 100 citrus accessions were sequenced on the Illumina platform with an average depth of 30-fold genome coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 23</a>). In addition, we used sequence data from three mandarins, one orange and four pummelos from previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Xu, Q. et al. The draft genome of sweet orange (Citrus sinensis). Nat. Genet. 45, 59–66 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2349">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Wu, G.A. et al. Sequencing of diverse mandarin, pummelo and orange genomes reveals complex history of admixture during citrus domestication. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 656–662 (2014)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2352">19</a></sup> for our analysis. The paired-end data were aligned to the pummelo reference genome using BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows–Wheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754–1760 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2356">53</a></sup>. Population-based SNP calling was performed using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Li, H. et al. The Sequence Alignment/Map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078–2079 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2360">54</a></sup>. To evaluate the relationships among the 108 accessions, a neighbor-joining tree was constructed using the maximum composite likelihood method in MEGA5 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Tamura, K. et al. MEGA5: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis using maximum likelihood, evolutionary distance, and maximum parsimony methods. Mol. Biol. Evol. 28, 2731–2739 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2364">55</a></sup> with 1,000 bootstrap test (data in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM105">Supplementary Data Set 3</a>). The shotgun genome sequencing data of 15 atalantia, 11 papeda and 19 pummelo were used to represent primitive, wild and cultivated citrus. The SNPs located in the regions of single-copy orthologous genes shared by the six citrus genomes were used to construct a phylogenetic tree using the substitution model GTRGAMMA of the maximum likelihood method with the RAxML software (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM106">Supplementary Data Set 4</a>).</p><p>For genetic diversity and selection signature analysis, SNPs of the whole genome were used, and a sliding-window approach (50-kb windows sliding in 10-kb steps) was employed to quantify genetic differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) and nucleotide diversity (π) for each pair of citrus populations using the VCFtools software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156–2158 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2382">56</a></sup>. Values of π were also calculated in 2-Mb sliding windows with steps sizes of 1 Mb. To detect the selection signatures, the log<sub>10</sub> π ratio was calculated. The regions with significantly high <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values (in the 5% right tail of the empirical distribution of <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> values) and significant reduction in diversity (in the 5% right tail of the empirical distribution of log<sub>10</sub> π ratio) were considered to be under selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 8</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Bulk segregant analysis (BSA) and pool sequencing.</h3><p>Two DNA pools from 20 individuals exhibiting extreme monoembryonic and polyembryonic phenotypes were collected from a population derived from an HB pummelo × Fairchild mandarin cross (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 29</a>). Sequencing was performed at tenfold and 60-fold depth for parents and DNA pools, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 40</a>). Reads from the parents and from the monoembryonic and polyembryonic pools were separately mapped to the pummelo genome using BWA (mapping quality &gt; 30). The SAMtools software was used for SNP calling (Q &gt; 30).</p><p>According to the genotypes of HB pummelo (monoembryonic, <i>pp</i>) and Fairchild mandarin (polyembryonic, <i>Pp</i>), the allelic nucleotide polymorphisms fitting <i>Pp</i> in the polyembryonic pool and <i>pp</i> in the monoembryonic pool were identified. The sites were retained if they fit the following criteria: <i>p</i>% of the monoembryonic pool ≥ 0.9 and 0.4 ≤ <i>p</i>% ≤ 0.6 in the polyembryonic pool. The absolute value of their difference was calculated as a Δ(SNP index). Using the sliding-window approach, the average Δ(SNP index) and max–min normalized SNP density were calculated with 250-kb sliding windows with 10-kb step sizes. The final transformed Δ(SNP index) was the product of the average Δ(SNP index) and the normalized SNP density.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Local gene-based association analysis for the polyembryony locus.</h3><p>The candidate polyembryony interval of 1.96 Mb was extracted from the genome sequences of atalantia, papeda, citron, sweet orange, and clementine mandarin by separately searching each genome with BLASTN. BWA was used to align the sequencing reads from the 108 accessions to the corresponding target sequences. The reads of each accession that mapped to the 1.96 Mb region were identified using the bam2fastq software (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Sec24">URLs</a>). These reads were remapped to gene sequences using the pummelo candidate regions as templates. After using iCORN2 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Otto, T.D., Sanders, M., Berriman, M. &amp; Newbold, C. Iterative Correction of Reference Nucleotides (iCORN) using second generation sequencing technology. Bioinformatics 26, 1704–1707 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2448">57</a>) to substitute SNPs and small indels (1–3 bp), we obtained 292 gene sequences in the candidate region of each accession.</p><p>For each of the 292 genes in the candidate region, a multiple sequence alignment of homologous sequences from 108 accessions was performed using ClustalW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Larkin, M.A. et al. Clustal W and Clustal X version 2.0. Bioinformatics 23, 2947–2948 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2455">58</a></sup>. For each polymorphic site, the <i>P</i> value from the two-tailed Fisher's exact test was calculated to quantify the associations between the nucleotide polymorphisms and the polyembryonic trait. The <i>P</i> values were adjusted using the FDR correction for multiple testing. The sites were retained if they fit the criteria of <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01 and FDR &lt; 0.01 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 9</a>). For the 11 candidate genes in the 80-kb region, all of the SNPs in the gene sequence from the 108 accessions were extracted and analyzed. For each SNP, the proportion of the accessions in which the genotype of the site was associated with the phenotype of the embryo was regarded as an association score. The SNPs with association scores greater than 80% were used for further SNP association analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">MITE marker development.</h3><p>The 203-bp MITE insertion is located on chromosome 4 of the pummelo genome between nucleotides 24,610,316 and 24,610,317, which is in the promoter region of <i>CitRWP</i>. Two pairs of primers ('mite_p1' and 'mite_p2', <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 41</a>) were designed. Primer pair 'mite_p1' contains degenerate nucleotide to be used for the broad germplasm of 786 accessions. Primer pair 'mite_p2' was specifically designed to be used for the progeny from the HB pummelo × Fairchild mandarin cross. Both the primer pairs anneal approximately 100 bp upstream (forward primer) and 200 bp downstream (reversed primer) of the MITE insertion site (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 10</a>). We performed a PCR-based screen to detect the MITE insertion. PCR was carried out in 20-μl reaction volumes containing 10 μl of 2× Taq Plus Mix (Vazyme Biotech), 100 ng genome template, 0.2 mM of each forward and reverse primer. The amplification was done at 95 °C for 5 min, followed by 30 cycles of 30 s at 95 °C, 30 s at 52 °C, and 60 s at 72 °C, and finally 5 min at 72 °C.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Transcriptome sequencing and analysis.</h3><p>A total of 26 samples from leaves, ovules, fruits, and seeds were harvested for RNA-seq analysis. Two replicates of plant tissue were analyzed. RNA-seq libraries were constructed and sequenced on Illumina Genome Analyzer platform. An average of 4 Gb data were generated for each sample (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Table 42</a>). The RNA-seq reads were aligned to the pummelo genome sequences using TopHat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2503">59</a></sup>. The expression level of each predicted transcript in each RNA-seq library was calculated as the FPKM with Cufflinks<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Transcript assembly and quantification by RNA-seq reveals unannotated transcripts and isoform switching during cell differentiation. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 511–515 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2507">60</a></sup>. Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis was performed using the web-based agriGO program with the default parameters (<i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 and FDR &lt; 0.05, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">Supplementary Tables 33, 34</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM107">43</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Note 11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Quantitative PCR analysis.</h3><p>Total RNA from all the tissues was extracted using the TRIzol reagent (Takara). cDNA was synthesized using 1 μg total RNA and the HiScript II QRT SuperMix for qPCR (Vazyme, R223-01). qRT-PCR was performed on an ABI 7900 instrument (Applied Biosystems) using SYBR Green PCR Mastermix according to the manufacturer's instructions (Kapa, RR420). The cycling conditions included incubation for 3 min at 95 °C followed by 40 cycles of amplification (95 °C for 5 s and 60 °C for 35 s). Using the citrus β-actin gene as the internal reference gene, relative gene expression values were calculated using the 2<sup>−ΔΔCt</sup> method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Livak, K.J. &amp; Schmittgen, T.D. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2−ΔΔCT method. Methods 25, 402–408 (2001)." href="/articles/ng.3839#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d38365e2535">61</a></sup>. The primers used in qRT-PCR are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.3839#MOESM102">Supplementary Table 41</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Statistical analyses.</h3><p>We used the Fisher's exact test to conduct the GO enrichment analysis of the target genes relative to the background of the corresponding entire genome using agriGO program. The likelihood ratio test was used to determine the branch model during the dN/dS analysis using PAML program. The two-tailed Fisher's exact tests were used to quantify the associations between the nucleotide polymorphisms and the polyembryonic trait. Moreover, all the <i>P</i> values from the tests mentioned above were adjusted using the FDR correction for multiple testing. The filtering criteria of <i>P</i> &lt; 0.05 and FDR &lt; 0.05 were given for the analysis of GO enrichment analysis and likelihood ratio test, and the filtering criteria were <i>P</i> &lt; 0.01 and FDR &lt; 0.01 for the associations analysis of citrus polyembryony.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Data availability.</h3><p>Genome assembly data for <i>Citrus grandis, C. ichangensis, C. medica</i> and <i>A. buxifolia</i> have been deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under accession numbers <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYQ00000000">MKYQ00000000</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYP00000000">MKYP00000000</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYO00000000">MKYO00000000</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYR00000000">MKYR00000000</a>, respectively. The versions described in this paper are <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYQ01000000">MKYQ01000000</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYP01000000">MKYP01000000</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYO01000000">MKYO01000000</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=MKYR01000000">MKYR01000000</a>, respectively. Whole-genome sequencing data for <i>C. grandis, C. ichangensis, C. medica, A. buxifolia, C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i> have been deposited in BioProject under accession numbers <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA318855">PRJNA318855</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA321657">PRJNA321657</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA320023">PRJNA320023</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA327148">PRJNA327148</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA320985">PRJNA320985</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA321100">PRJNA321100</a>, respectively. The RNA-seq data for <i>C. grandis, C. ichangensis, C. medica, A. buxifolia, C. reticulata</i> and <i>C. sinensis</i> have been deposited in BioProject under accession codes <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA339650">PRJNA339650</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA340932">PRJNA340932</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA341284">PRJNA341284</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA341533">PRJNA341533</a>, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA341756">PRJNA341756</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA340305">PRJNA340305</a>. The data for genome assembly and annotation of <i>C. grandis, C. ichangensis, C. medica</i> and <i>A. buxifolia</i> are available through our website at <a href="http://citrus.hzau.edu.cn/orange/download/index.php">http://citrus.hzau.edu.cn/orange/download/index.php</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">URLs.</h3><p>PlnTFDB database, <a href="http://plntfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v3.0/">http://plntfdb.bio.uni-potsdam.de/v3.0/</a>; Pfam, <a href="http://pfam.xfam.org/browse">http://pfam.xfam.org/browse</a>; PROSITE, <a href="http://prosite.expasy.org/prosite.html">http://prosite.expasy.org/prosite.html</a>; bam2fastq, <a href="https://gsl.hudsonalpha.org/information/software/bam2fastq">https://gsl.hudsonalpha.org/information/software/bam2fastq</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec25" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec25-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec25">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec25-content"><p><b>Publisher's note:</b> Springer Nature remains neutral with regard to jurisdictional claims in published maps and institutional affiliations.</p></div></div></section>

                        <span class="c-article-identifiers"><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/entrez/query.fcgi?cmd=search&amp;term=Qiang+Xu" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - pubmed" data-track-label="link">PubMed</a><span class="u-hide"> </span><a class="c-article-identifiers__item" href="http://scholar.google.co.uk/scholar?as_q=&amp;num=10&amp;btnG=Search+Scholar&amp;as_epq=&amp;as_oq=&amp;as_eq=&amp;as_occt=any&amp;as_sauthors=%22Qiang+Xu%22&amp;as_publication=&amp;as_ylo=&amp;as_yhi=&amp;as_allsubj=all&amp;hl=en" data-track="click" data-track-action="author link - scholar" data-track-label="link">Google Scholar</a></span></p></div></div></li></ol></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="contributions">Contributions</h3><p>Q.X. conceived and designed the project and the strategy. X.W. performed comparative and population genomics and genetic mapping analyses and annotated three draft genomes. Y.X. performed transcriptome and genetic analyses. S.Z analyzed candidate genes and molecular markers in the populations. L.C. provided the haploid pummelo samples. Y.H. assembled the haploid genome and managed the database. J.C. and S.T. assembled the draft genomes. G.W. performed the genome annotations and anchor. Y.L. annotated one draft genome. C.C. and H.L. performed cytological analyses. H.Y., L.W., X.J., X.Y. and J.X. collected and evaluated the samples for genome and transcriptome analyses. Q.X. coordinated the project with help from X.D., B.-G.M., L.-L.C. and R.M.L. Z.X. and M.T. generated and maintained the segregating population for this study. X.W., Y.X. and Q.X. wrote the manuscript with contributions from R.M.L., S.Z., Y.H., J.C., G.W., S.T., C.C., Y.L., N.W., L.-L.C., B.-G.M., Y.R. and X.D.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/ng.3839/email/correspondent/c1/new">Qiang Xu</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading">Competing interests</h3>
